<p>Dr. Dre made his first on screen appearance as a weapons dealer in the 1996 bank robbery movie <i><a href="/wiki/Set_It_Off_(film)" title="Set It Off (film)">Set It Off</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> In 2001, Dr.&#160;Dre also appeared in the movies <i><a href="/wiki/The_Wash_(2001_film)" title="The Wash (2001 film)">The Wash</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Training_Day" title="Training Day">Training Day</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Dr._Dre&#39;s_Final_Album_Will_Be_Hip-Hop_Musical_68-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dr._Dre&#39;s_Final_Album_Will_Be_Hip-Hop_Musical-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> A song of his, "Bad Intentions" (featuring <a href="/wiki/Knoc-Turn%27Al" class="mw-redirect" title="Knoc-Turn&#39;Al">Knoc-Turn'Al</a> and produced by Mahogany), was featured on <a href="/wiki/The_Wash_(soundtrack)" title="The Wash (soundtrack)"><i>The Wash</i> soundtrack</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> Dr.&#160;Dre also appeared on two other songs "On the Blvd." and "The Wash" along with his co-star Snoop Dogg. 
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Crucial_Films">Crucial Films</span></h3>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1066479718"><table class="infobox vcard"><caption class="infobox-title fn org">Crucial Films</caption><tbody><tr><td colspan="2" class="infobox-image logo"><span class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Frameless"><a href="/wiki/File:Dr._Dre_logo_b.png" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Dr._Dre_logo_b.png/220px-Dr._Dre_logo_b.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="35" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Dr._Dre_logo_b.png/330px-Dr._Dre_logo_b.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Dr._Dre_logo_b.png/440px-Dr._Dre_logo_b.png 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="96" /></a></span></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label" style="padding-right: 0.5em;">Industry</th><td class="infobox-data category" style="line-height: 1.35em;">Film production company</td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label" style="padding-right: 0.5em;">Founded</th><td class="infobox-data" style="line-height: 1.35em;">2007</td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label" style="padding-right: 0.5em;">Founder</th><td class="infobox-data agent" style="line-height: 1.35em;">Dr. Dre</td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label" style="padding-right: 0.5em;">Fate</th><td class="infobox-data" style="line-height: 1.35em;">Inactive</td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label" style="padding-right: 0.5em;">Owner</th><td class="infobox-data" style="line-height: 1.35em;"><a href="/wiki/New_Line_Cinema" title="New Line Cinema">New Line Cinema</a></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label" style="padding-right: 0.5em;">Website</th><td class="infobox-data" style="line-height: 1.35em;"><span class="url"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://crucialfilms.com">crucialfilms<wbr />.com</a></span></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>In February 2007, it was announced that Dr.&#160;Dre would produce dark comedies and horror films for <a href="/wiki/New_Line_Cinema" title="New Line Cinema">New Line Cinema</a>-owned company Crucial Films, along with longtime video director <a href="/wiki/Phillip_Atwell" class="mw-redirect" title="Phillip Atwell">Phillip Atwell</a>. Dr.&#160;Dre announced "This is a natural switch for me, since I've directed a lot of music videos, and I eventually want to get into directing."<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> Along with fellow member Ice Cube, Dr. Dre produced <i><a href="/wiki/Straight_Outta_Compton_(2015_film)" class="mw-redirect" title="Straight Outta Compton (2015 film)">Straight Outta Compton</a></i> (2015), a biographical film about N.W.A.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Entrepreneurship">Entrepreneurship</span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Beats_Electronics">Beats Electronics</span></h4>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Beats_by_dre.PNG" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Beats_by_dre.PNG/220px-Beats_by_dre.PNG" decoding="async" width="220" height="33" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Beats_by_dre.PNG/330px-Beats_by_dre.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Beats_by_dre.PNG/440px-Beats_by_dre.PNG 2x" data-file-width="503" data-file-height="76" /></a><figcaption>Beats by Dr. Dre logo</figcaption></figure>
<p>In 2006, Dre co-founded Beats Electronics with his partner, Jimmy Iovine.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> Its first brand of headphones were launched in July 2008. The line consisted of Beats Studio, a <a href="/wiki/Circumaural" class="mw-redirect" title="Circumaural">circumaural</a> headphone; Beats Tour, an in-ear headphone; Beats Solo &amp; Solo HD, a <a href="/wiki/Supra-aural" class="mw-redirect" title="Supra-aural">supra-aural</a> headphone; Beats Spin; Heartbeats by <a href="/wiki/Lady_Gaga" title="Lady Gaga">Lady Gaga</a>, also an in-ear headphone; and <a href="/wiki/Sean_Combs" title="Sean Combs">Diddy</a> Beats.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> In late 2009, <a href="/wiki/Hewlett-Packard" title="Hewlett-Packard">Hewlett-Packard</a> participated in a deal to bundle Beats By Dr. Dre with some HP laptops and headsets.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> HP and Dr. Dre announced the deal on October 9, 2009, at a press event. An exclusive laptop, known as the HP ENVY 15 Beats limited edition, was released for sale October 22. In January 2014, Beats Music was introduced and launched as a streaming service.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup> Then, in May, technology giant Apple purchased the Beats brand for $3.4 billion.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> The deal made Dr. Dre the "richest man in hip hop".<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup> Dr. Dre became an Apple employee in an executive role,<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> and worked with Apple for years.<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> As of 2022, it was found that Apple had subtracted $200 million from the deal after entertainer <a href="/wiki/Tyrese_Gibson" title="Tyrese Gibson">Tyrese Gibson</a> revealed the news of the acquisition on social media a month before it was completed without the company's permission.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Philanthropy">Philanthropy</span></h3>
<p>During May 2013, Dr. Dre and <a href="/wiki/Jimmy_Iovine" title="Jimmy Iovine">Jimmy Iovine</a> donated a $70 million endowment to the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Southern_California" title="University of Southern California">University of Southern California</a> to create the USC Jimmy Iovine and Andre Young Academy for Arts, Technology and the Business of Innovation. The goal of the academy has been stated as "to shape the future by nurturing the talents, passions, leadership and risk-taking of uniquely qualified students who are motivated to explore and create new art forms, technologies, and business models." The first class of the academy began in September 2014.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In June 2017, it was announced that Dr. Dre has committed $10 million to the construction of a performing arts center for the new <a href="/wiki/Compton_High_School" title="Compton High School">Compton High School</a>. The center will encompass creative resources and a 1,200-seat theater, and is expected to break ground in 2020. The project is a partnership between Dr. Dre and the Compton Unified School District.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Commercial_endorsements">Commercial endorsements</span></h3>
<p>In 2002 and 2003, Dr. Dre appeared in TV commercials for <a href="/wiki/Coors_Light" title="Coors Light">Coors Light</a> beer.<sup id="cite_ref-AdForum_143-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AdForum-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Beginning in 2009, Dr. Dre appeared in TV commercials that also featured his <a href="/wiki/Beats_Electronics" title="Beats Electronics">Beats Electronics</a> product line. A 2009  commercial for the <a href="/wiki/Dr_Pepper" title="Dr Pepper">Dr Pepper</a> soft drink had Dr. Dre DJing with Beats headphones and playing a brief snippet off the never-released <i>Detox</i> album.<sup id="cite_ref-Billboard_Dr_Pepper_79-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Billboard_Dr_Pepper-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Dr_Pepper_ad_80-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dr_Pepper_ad-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> In 2010, Dr. Dre had a cameo in a commercial for <a href="/wiki/HP_Inc." title="HP Inc.">HP</a> laptops that featured a plug for Beats Audio.<sup id="cite_ref-AdForum_143-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AdForum-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> Then in 2011, the <a href="/wiki/Chrysler_300" title="Chrysler 300">Chrysler 300S</a> "Imported from Detroit" ad campaign had a commercial narrated by Dr. Dre and including a plug for Beats Audio.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dr._Dre_started_Burning_Man_rumors">Dr. Dre started Burning Man rumors</span></h3>
<p>An urban legend surfaced in 2011 when a <a href="/wiki/Tumblr" title="Tumblr">Tumblr</a> blog titled Dr. Dre Started Burning Man<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> began promulgating the notion that the producer, rapper and entrepreneur had discovered <a href="/wiki/Burning_Man" title="Burning Man">Burning Man</a> in 1995 during a music video shoot and offered to cover the cost of the event's permit from the Nevada Bureau of Land Management under an agreement with the festival's organizers that he could institute an entrance fee system, which had not existed before his participation.<sup id="cite_ref-salon.com_147-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-salon.com-147">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup> This claim was supported by an alleged letter from Dre to Nicole Threatt Young that indicated that Dre had shared his experience witnessing the Burning Man festival with her.<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><i><a href="/wiki/Business_Insider" title="Business Insider">Business Insider</a></i> mentions the portion of the letter where Dr. Dre purportedly states "someone should get behind this&#160;... and make some money off these fools" and compares Dr. Dre's potential entrepreneurial engagement with Burning Man as a parallel to <a href="/wiki/Steve_Jobs" title="Steve Jobs">Steve Jobs</a>' efforts to centralize and profit from the otherwise unorganized online music industry.<sup id="cite_ref-Business_Insider_Burning_Man_151-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Business_Insider_Burning_Man-151">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/Salon_(website)" class="mw-redirect" title="Salon (website)"><i>Salon</i></a>, Dr. Dre's ethos seems to be aligned with seven of the ten principles of the Burning Man community: "radical self-reliance, radical self-expression, communal effort, civic responsibility, leaving no trace, participation and immediacy."<sup id="cite_ref-salon.com_147-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-salon.com-147">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Musical_influences_and_style">Musical influences and style</span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>The space, about the size of a college dorm room, is splattered with papers, ideas scribbled down in black ink. Nuthin' but G thangs waiting to happen. Those that don't happen end up in a round, purple <a href="/wiki/L.A._Lakers" class="mw-redirect" title="L.A. Lakers">L.A. Lakers</a> trash can. A kitchen, red and stainless steel like a '50s diner, adjoins the control room </p><div class="templatequotecite">—&#8202;<cite>Corey Moss of <a href="/wiki/MTV_News" title="MTV News">MTV News</a>, in a 2002 profile of Dr. Dre with a visit to his studio<sup id="cite_ref-Dre_at_home_1_152-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dre_at_home_1-152">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup></cite></div></blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Production_style">Production style</span></h3>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Dr._Dre_in_2011.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Dr._Dre_in_2011.jpg/170px-Dr._Dre_in_2011.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="256" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/de/Dr._Dre_in_2011.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="224" data-file-height="337" /></a><figcaption>Dre in 2011</figcaption></figure>
<p>Dre is noted for his evolving production style, while always keeping in touch with his early musical sound and re-shaping elements from previous work. At the beginning of his career as a producer for the <a href="/wiki/World_Class_Wreckin_Cru" class="mw-redirect" title="World Class Wreckin Cru">World Class Wreckin Cru</a> with DJ Alonzo Williams in the mid-1980s, his music was in the <a href="/wiki/Electro-hop" class="mw-redirect" title="Electro-hop">electro-hop</a> style pioneered by <a href="/wiki/The_Unknown_DJ" title="The Unknown DJ">the Unknown DJ</a>, and that of early hip-hop groups like the <a href="/wiki/Beastie_Boys" title="Beastie Boys">Beastie Boys</a> and <a href="/wiki/Whodini" title="Whodini">Whodini</a>.
</p><p>In 1987, Dr. Dre sampled the <a href="/wiki/Ohio_Players" title="Ohio Players">Ohio Players</a>' ARP synth riffs from their 1973 funk hit "<a href="/wiki/Funky_Worm" title="Funky Worm">Funky Worm</a>" in the N.W.A song "Dopeman". Being the first hip-hop producer to sample the song,<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup> Dre both paved the way for the future popularization of the G-funk style within hip-hop, and established heavy synthesizer solos as an integral part of his production style. Dr. Dre was also one of the very first producers to interpolate the then little-known <a href="/wiki/Drum_break" class="mw-redirect" title="Drum break">drum break</a> from <a href="/wiki/The_Winstons" title="The Winstons">The Winstons</a>' "Amen, Brother" in the N.W.A song "<a href="/wiki/Straight_Outta_Compton_(song)" title="Straight Outta Compton (song)">Straight Outta Compton</a>". This break has since becοme a staple in not only hip-hop, but all popular music, having been used in over 1700 songs.<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>From <i><a href="/wiki/Straight_Outta_Compton" title="Straight Outta Compton">Straight Outta Compton</a></i> on, Dre uses live musicians to replay old melodies rather than sampling them. With <a href="/wiki/Ruthless_Records" title="Ruthless Records">Ruthless Records</a>, collaborators included guitarist Mike "Crazy Neck" Sims, multi-instrumentalist Colin Wolfe, <a href="/wiki/DJ_Yella" title="DJ Yella">DJ Yella</a> and sound engineer Donovan "The Dirt Biker" Sound. Dre is receptive of new ideas from other producers, one example being his fruitful collaboration with <a href="/wiki/Above_the_Law_(group)" title="Above the Law (group)">Above the Law</a>'s producer <a href="/wiki/Cold_187um" title="Cold 187um">Cold 187um</a> while at Ruthless. Cold 187&#160;um was at the time experimenting with 1970s <a href="/wiki/P-Funk" class="mw-redirect" title="P-Funk">P-Funk</a> samples (<a href="/wiki/Parliament_(band)" title="Parliament (band)">Parliament</a>, <a href="/wiki/Funkadelic" title="Funkadelic">Funkadelic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bootsy_Collins" title="Bootsy Collins">Bootsy Collins</a>, <a href="/wiki/George_Clinton_(funk_musician)" title="George Clinton (funk musician)">George Clinton</a> etc.), that Dre also used. Dre has since been accused of "stealing" the concept of <a href="/wiki/G-funk" title="G-funk">G-funk</a> from Cold 187&#160;um.<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Upon leaving Ruthless and forming <a href="/wiki/Death_Row_Records" title="Death Row Records">Death Row Records</a> in 1991, Dre called on veteran West Coast DJ <a href="/wiki/Chris_%22The_Glove%22_Taylor" class="mw-redirect" title="Chris &quot;The Glove&quot; Taylor">Chris "the Glove" Taylor</a> and sound engineer Greg "Gregski" Royal, along with Colin Wolfe, to help him on future projects. His 1992 album <i><a href="/wiki/The_Chronic" title="The Chronic">The Chronic</a></i> is thought to be one of the most well-produced hip-hop albums of all time.<sup id="cite_ref-RapCentral_156-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RapCentral-156">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BBC25Years_158-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC25Years-158">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup> Musical themes included hard-hitting synthesizer solos played by Wolfe, bass-heavy compositions, background female vocals and Dre fully embracing 1970s funk samples. Dre used a <a href="/wiki/Minimoog" title="Minimoog">minimoog</a> synth to replay the melody from <a href="/wiki/Leon_Haywood" title="Leon Haywood">Leon Haywood</a>'s 1972 song "I Wanna Do Somethin' Freaky to You" for the Chronic's first single "<a href="/wiki/Nuthin%27_but_a_%22G%22_Thang" class="mw-redirect" title="Nuthin&#39; but a &quot;G&quot; Thang">Nuthin' but a 'G' Thang</a>" which became a global hit. For his new protégé <a href="/wiki/Snoop_Doggy_Dogg" class="mw-redirect" title="Snoop Doggy Dogg">Snoop Doggy Dogg</a>'s album <i><a href="/wiki/Doggystyle" title="Doggystyle">Doggystyle</a></i>, Dre collaborated with then 19-year-old producer <a href="/wiki/Daz_Dillinger" title="Daz Dillinger">Daz Dillinger</a>, who received co-production credits on songs "Serial Killa" and "For all My Niggaz &amp; Bitches", <a href="/wiki/The_Dramatics" title="The Dramatics">The Dramatics</a> bass player <a href="/wiki/Edward_Tony_Green" title="Edward Tony Green">Tony "T. Money" Green</a>, guitarist Ricky Rouse, keyboardists Emanuel "Porkchop" Dean and Sean "Barney Rubble" Thomas and engineer Tommy Daugherty, as well as <a href="/wiki/Warren_G" title="Warren G">Warren G</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sam_Sneed" title="Sam Sneed">Sam Sneed</a>, who are credited with bringing several samples to the studio.<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The influence of <i>The Chronic</i> and <i>Doggystyle</i> on the popular music of the 1990s went not only far beyond the West Coast, but beyond hip-hop as a genre. Artists as diverse as <a href="/wiki/Master_P" title="Master P">Master P</a> ("Bout It, Bout It"), <a href="/wiki/George_Michael" title="George Michael">George Michael</a> ("<a href="/wiki/Fastlove" title="Fastlove">Fastlove</a>"), <a href="/wiki/Mariah_Carey" title="Mariah Carey">Mariah Carey</a> ("<a href="/wiki/Fantasy_(Mariah_Carey_song)" title="Fantasy (Mariah Carey song)">Fantasy</a>"), <a href="/wiki/Adina_Howard" title="Adina Howard">Adina Howard</a> ("<a href="/wiki/Freak_Like_Me" class="mw-redirect" title="Freak Like Me">Freak Like Me</a>"), <a href="/wiki/Luis_Miguel" title="Luis Miguel">Luis Miguel</a> ("Dame"), and <a href="/wiki/The_Spice_Girls" class="mw-redirect" title="The Spice Girls">The Spice Girls</a> ("<a href="/wiki/Say_You%27ll_Be_There" title="Say You&#39;ll Be There">Say You'll Be There</a>") used G-funk instrumentation in their songs.<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bad_Boy_Records" title="Bad Boy Records">Bad Boy Records</a> producer <a href="/wiki/Chucky_Thompson" title="Chucky Thompson">Chucky Thompson</a> stated in the April 2004 issue of <i><a href="/wiki/XXL_(magazine)" title="XXL (magazine)">XXL</a></i> magazine that the sound of <i>Doggystyle</i> and <i>The Chronic</i> was the basis for the <a href="/wiki/Notorious_B.I.G." class="mw-redirect" title="Notorious B.I.G.">Notorious B.I.G.</a>'s 1995 hit single "<a href="/wiki/Big_Poppa" title="Big Poppa">Big Poppa</a>":
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942"><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>At that time, we were listening to Snoop's album. We knew what was going on in the West through Dr. Dre. Big just knew the culture, he knew what was going on with hip-hop. It was more than just New York, it was all over.<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>In 1994, starting with the <i><a href="/wiki/Murder_was_the_Case" class="mw-redirect" title="Murder was the Case">Murder was the Case</a></i> soundtrack, Dre attempted to push the boundaries of G-funk further into a darker sound. In songs such as "Murder was the Case" and "<a href="/wiki/Natural_Born_Killaz" title="Natural Born Killaz">Natural Born Killaz</a>", the synthesizer pitch is higher and the drum tempo is slowed down to 91 BPM<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup> (87 BPM in the remix) to create a dark and gritty atmosphere. <a href="/wiki/Percussion" class="mw-redirect" title="Percussion">Percussion</a> instruments, particularly <a href="/wiki/Sleigh_bell" class="mw-redirect" title="Sleigh bell">sleigh bells</a>, are also present. Dre's frequent collaborators from this period included <a href="/wiki/Pittsburgh,_Pennsylvania" class="mw-redirect" title="Pittsburgh, Pennsylvania">Pittsburgh, Pennsylvania</a> natives Stuart "Stu-B-Doo" Bullard, a multi-instrumentalist from the Ozanam Strings Orchestra,<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Sam_Sneed" title="Sam Sneed">Sam Sneed</a>, Stephen "<a href="/wiki/Bud%27da" title="Bud&#39;da">Bud'da</a>" Anderson,<sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup> and percussionist Carl "Butch" Small. This style of production has been influential far beyond the West Coast. The beat for the <a href="/wiki/Houston" title="Houston">Houston</a>-based group <a href="/wiki/Geto_Boys" title="Geto Boys">Geto Boys</a> 1996 song "Still" follows the same drum pattern as "Natural Born Killaz" and Eazy E's "Wut Would U Do" (a diss to Dre) is similar to the original "Murder was the Case" instrumental. This style of production is usually accompanied by <a href="/wiki/Horror_fiction" title="Horror fiction">horror</a> and <a href="/wiki/Occult" title="Occult">occult</a>-themed lyrics and imagery, being crucial to the creation of <a href="/wiki/Horrorcore" title="Horrorcore">horrorcore</a>.
</p><p>By 1996, Dre was again looking to innovate his sound. He recruited keyboardist Camara Kambon to play the keys on "<a href="/wiki/Been_There,_Done_That_(Dr._Dre_song)" title="Been There, Done That (Dr. Dre song)">Been There, Done That</a>", and through Bud'da and Sam Sneed he was introduced to fellow Pittsburgh native <a href="/wiki/Melvin_%22Mel-Man%22_Bradford" class="mw-redirect" title="Melvin &quot;Mel-Man&quot; Bradford">Melvin "Mel-Man" Bradford</a>. At this time, he also switched from using the E-mu SP-1200 to the Akai MPC3000 drum kit and sampler, which he still uses today. Beginning with his 1996 compilation <a href="/wiki/Dr._Dre_Presents_the_Aftermath" class="mw-redirect" title="Dr. Dre Presents the Aftermath">Dr. Dre Presents the Aftermath</a>, Dre's production has taken a less sample-based approach, with loud, layered <a href="/wiki/Snare_drum" title="Snare drum">snare drums</a> dominating the mix, while synthesizers are still omnipresent. In his critically acclaimed second album, <a href="/wiki/2001_(Dr._Dre_album)" title="2001 (Dr. Dre album)">2001</a>, live instrumentation takes the place of sampling, a famous example being "<a href="/wiki/The_Next_Episode" title="The Next Episode">The Next Episode</a>", in which keyboardist Camara Kambon re-played live the main melody from <a href="/wiki/David_McCallum" title="David McCallum">David McCallum</a>'s 1967 jazz-funk work "The Edge". For every song on <i>2001</i>, Dre had a <a href="/wiki/Keyboardist" title="Keyboardist">keyboardist</a>, <a href="/wiki/Guitarist" title="Guitarist">guitarist</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bassist" title="Bassist">bassist</a> create the basic parts of the beat, while he himself programmed the <a href="/wiki/Drums" class="mw-redirect" title="Drums">drums</a>, did the <a href="/wiki/Music_sequencer" title="Music sequencer">sequencing</a> and <a href="/wiki/Overdubbing" title="Overdubbing">overdubbing</a> and added sound effects, and later mixed the songs. During this period, Dre's signature "west coast whistle" riffs are still present albeit in a lower pitch, as in "Light Speed", "Housewife", "Some L.A. Niggaz" and Eminem's "<a href="/wiki/Guilty_Conscience_(song)" title="Guilty Conscience (song)">Guilty Conscience</a>" hook. The sound of "2001" had tremendous influence on hip-hop production, redefining the West Coast's sound and expanding the G-funk of the early 1990s. To produce the album, Dre and Mel-Man relied on the talents of <a href="/wiki/Scott_Storch" title="Scott Storch">Scott Storch</a> and Camara Kambon on the keys, <a href="/wiki/Mike_Elizondo" title="Mike Elizondo">Mike Elizondo</a> and Colin Wolfe on bass guitar, Sean Cruse on lead guitar and sound engineers Richard "Segal" Huredia and Mauricio "Veto" Iragorri.<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>From the mid-2000s, Dr. Dre has taken on a more soulful production style, using more of a classical piano instead of a keyboard, and having <a href="/wiki/Clapping" title="Clapping">claps</a> replace snares, as evidenced in songs such as Snoop Dogg's "Imagine" and "<a href="/wiki/Boss%27_Life" title="Boss&#39; Life">Boss' Life</a>", <a href="/wiki/Busta_Rhymes" title="Busta Rhymes">Busta Rhymes</a>' "Get You Some" and "Been Through the Storm", <a href="/wiki/Stat_Quo" title="Stat Quo">Stat Quo</a>'s "Get Low" and "The Way It Be", Jay-Z's "<a href="/wiki/Lost_One" title="Lost One">Lost One</a>", Nas' "Hustlers", and several beats on Eminem's Relapse album. Soul and R&amp;B pianist <a href="/wiki/Mark_Batson" title="Mark Batson">Mark Batson</a>, having previously worked with <a href="/wiki/The_Dave_Matthews_Band" class="mw-redirect" title="The Dave Matthews Band">The Dave Matthews Band</a>, <a href="/wiki/Seal_(musician)" title="Seal (musician)">Seal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Maroon_5" title="Maroon 5">Maroon 5</a> has been credited as the architect of this sound. Besides Batson, Aftermath producer and understudy of Dre's, <a href="/wiki/Dawaun_Parker" title="Dawaun Parker">Dawaun Parker</a>, who has named <a href="/wiki/Q-Tip_(musician)" title="Q-Tip (musician)">Q-Tip</a> and <a href="/wiki/J_Dilla" title="J Dilla">J Dilla</a> as his primary influences, is thought to be responsible for giving Dre's newest beats an East Coast feel.<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Despite an occasional hint of trap about the beats and an intriguingly warped use of autotune in his <i>Compton</i> song, "Darkside/Gone", his production seems to stand slightly apart from current trends in hip-hop like Eminem's song "Little Engine" with an ominous horrorcore beat — reminiscent of some of his works on Eminem's album Relapse – or the West Coast joint Lock It Up.<sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Production_equipment">Production equipment</span></h3>
<p>Dr.&#160;Dre has said that his primary instrument in the studio is the <a href="/wiki/Music_Production_Center" class="mw-redirect" title="Music Production Center">Akai MPC3000</a>, a drum machine and sampler, and that he often uses as many as four or five to produce a single recording. He cites 1970s <a href="/wiki/Funk" title="Funk">funk</a> musicians such as <a href="/wiki/George_Clinton_(funk_musician)" title="George Clinton (funk musician)">George Clinton</a>, <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Hayes" title="Isaac Hayes">Isaac Hayes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Curtis_Mayfield" title="Curtis Mayfield">Curtis Mayfield</a> as his primary musical influences. Unlike most rap producers, he tries to avoid samples as much as possible, preferring to have studio musicians re-play pieces of music he wants to use, because it allows him more flexibility to change the pieces in rhythm and tempo.<sup id="cite_ref-scratch_2004_170-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-scratch_2004-170">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup> In 2001 he told <i><a href="/wiki/Time_(magazine)" title="Time (magazine)">Time</a></i> magazine, "I may hear something I like on an old record that may inspire me, but I'd rather use musicians to re-create the sound or elaborate on it. I can control it better."<sup id="cite_ref-in_the_doctor&#39;s_house_171-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-in_the_doctor&#39;s_house-171">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Other equipment he uses includes the <a href="/wiki/E-mu_SP-1200" title="E-mu SP-1200">E-mu SP-1200</a> drum machine and other keyboards from such manufacturers as Korg, Rhodes, Wurlitzer, Moog, and Roland.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERo2007234_172-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERo2007234-172">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup> Dr. Dre also stresses the importance of <a href="/wiki/Equalization_(audio)" title="Equalization (audio)">equalizing</a> drums properly, telling <i><a href="/wiki/Scratch_(magazine)" title="Scratch (magazine)">Scratch</a></i> in 2004 that he "used the same drum sounds on a couple of different songs on one album before but you'd never be able to tell the difference because of the EQ".<sup id="cite_ref-scratch_2004_170-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-scratch_2004-170">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup>  Dr. Dre also uses the digital audio workstation <a href="/wiki/Pro_Tools" title="Pro Tools">Pro Tools</a> and uses the software to combine hardware drum machines and vintage analog keyboards and synthesizers.<sup id="cite_ref-scratch_2004_170-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-scratch_2004-170">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>After founding Aftermath Entertainment in 1996, Dr.&#160;Dre took on producer <a href="/wiki/Melvin_%22Mel-Man%22_Bradford" class="mw-redirect" title="Melvin &quot;Mel-Man&quot; Bradford">Mel-Man</a> as a co-producer, and his music took on a more synthesizer-based sound, using fewer vocal samples (as he had used on "Lil' Ghetto Boy" and "Let Me Ride" on <i>The Chronic</i>, for example). Mel-Man has not shared co-production credits with Dr.&#160;Dre since approximately 2002, but fellow Aftermath producer Focus has credited Mel-Man as a key architect of the signature Aftermath sound.<sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1999, Dr.&#160;Dre started working with <a href="/wiki/Mike_Elizondo" title="Mike Elizondo">Mike Elizondo</a>, a bassist, guitarist, and keyboardist who has also produced, written and played on records for female singers such as <a href="/wiki/Ann_Danielewski" class="mw-redirect" title="Ann Danielewski">Poe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fiona_Apple" title="Fiona Apple">Fiona Apple</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alanis_Morissette" title="Alanis Morissette">Alanis Morissette</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup> In the past few years Elizondo has since worked for many of Dr.&#160;Dre's productions.<sup id="cite_ref-shock_176-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-shock-176">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-songwriter_universe_177-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-songwriter_universe-177">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup> Dr.&#160;Dre also told <i>Scratch</i> magazine in a 2004 interview that he has been studying piano and music theory formally, and that a major goal is to accumulate enough musical theory to score movies. In the same interview he stated that he has collaborated with famed 1960s songwriter <a href="/wiki/Burt_Bacharach" title="Burt Bacharach">Burt Bacharach</a> by sending him hip hop beats to play over, and hopes to have an in-person collaboration with him in the future.<sup id="cite_ref-scratch_2004_170-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-scratch_2004-170">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Work_ethic">Work ethic</span></h3>
<p>Dr.&#160;Dre has stated that he is a perfectionist and is known to pressure the artists with whom he records to give flawless performances.<sup id="cite_ref-scratch_2004_170-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-scratch_2004-170">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup> In 2006, Snoop Dogg told the website Dubcnn.com that Dr.&#160;Dre had made new artist <a href="/wiki/Bishop_Lamont" title="Bishop Lamont">Bishop Lamont</a> re-record a single bar of vocals 107&#160;times.<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup> Dr.&#160;Dre has also stated that Eminem is a fellow perfectionist, and attributes his success on Aftermath to his similar work ethic.<sup id="cite_ref-scratch_2004_170-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-scratch_2004-170">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup> He gives a lot of input into the delivery of the vocals and will stop an <a href="/wiki/MC" class="mw-redirect" title="MC">MC</a> during a take if it is not to his liking.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEdwards2009285–286_179-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEdwards2009285–286-179">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup> However, he gives MCs that he works with room to write lyrics without too much instruction unless it is a specifically conceptual record, as noted by <a href="/wiki/Bishop_Lamont" title="Bishop Lamont">Bishop Lamont</a> in the book <i><a href="/wiki/How_to_Rap" title="How to Rap">How to Rap</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEdwards2009234–235_180-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEdwards2009234–235-180">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A consequence of his perfectionism is that some artists who initially sign deals with Dr.&#160;Dre's Aftermath label never release albums. In 2001, Aftermath released the soundtrack to the movie <i>The Wash</i>, featuring a number of Aftermath acts such as Shaunta, Daks, Joe Beast and Toi. To date, none have released full-length albums on Aftermath and have apparently ended their relationships with the label and Dr.&#160;Dre. Other noteworthy acts to leave Aftermath without releasing albums include <a href="/wiki/King_Tee" class="mw-redirect" title="King Tee">King Tee</a>, <i>2001</i> vocalist Hittman, <a href="/wiki/Joell_Ortiz" title="Joell Ortiz">Joell Ortiz</a>, <a href="/wiki/Raekwon" title="Raekwon">Raekwon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rakim" title="Rakim">Rakim</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-source_2003_181-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-source_2003-181">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Collaborators_and_co-producers">Collaborators and co-producers</span></h3>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Snoop_Dogg_and_Dr._Dre.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Snoop_Dogg_and_Dr._Dre.jpg/220px-Snoop_Dogg_and_Dr._Dre.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="146" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Snoop_Dogg_and_Dr._Dre.jpg/330px-Snoop_Dogg_and_Dr._Dre.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Snoop_Dogg_and_Dr._Dre.jpg/440px-Snoop_Dogg_and_Dr._Dre.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="680" /></a><figcaption>Dr. Dre performing with Snoop Dogg, 2012</figcaption></figure>
<p>Over the years, word of other collaborators who have contributed to Dr.&#160;Dre's work has surfaced. During his tenure at Death Row Records, it was alleged that Dr.&#160;Dre's stepbrother Warren G and <a href="/wiki/Tha_Dogg_Pound" title="Tha Dogg Pound">Tha Dogg Pound</a> member <a href="/wiki/Daz_Dillinger" title="Daz Dillinger">Daz</a> made many uncredited contributions to songs on his solo album <i>The Chronic</i> and Snoop Doggy Dogg's album <i><a href="/wiki/Doggystyle" title="Doggystyle">Doggystyle</a></i> (Daz received production credits on Snoop's similar-sounding, albeit less successful album <i>Tha Doggfather</i> after Young left Death Row Records).<sup id="cite_ref-DPG-Eulogy_182-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DPG-Eulogy-182">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>It is known that <a href="/wiki/Scott_Storch" title="Scott Storch">Scott Storch</a>, who has since gone on to become a successful producer in his own right, contributed to Dr.&#160;Dre's second album <i>2001</i>; Storch is credited as a songwriter on several songs and played keyboards on several tracks. In 2006 he told <i>Rolling Stone</i>:
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942"><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>"At the time, I saw Dr.&#160;Dre desperately needed something," Storch says. "He needed a fuel injection, and Dr.&#160;Dre utilized me as the nitrous oxide. He threw me into the mix, and I sort of tapped on a new flavor with my whole piano sound and the strings and orchestration. So I'd be on the keyboards, and Mike [Elizondo] was on the bass guitar, and Dr.&#160;Dre was on the drum machine".<sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>Current collaborator <a href="/wiki/Mike_Elizondo" title="Mike Elizondo">Mike Elizondo</a>, when speaking about his work with Young, describes their recording process as a collaborative effort involving several musicians. In 2004 he claimed to <i>Songwriter Universe</i> magazine that he had written the foundations of the hit Eminem song "<a href="/wiki/The_Real_Slim_Shady" title="The Real Slim Shady">The Real Slim Shady</a>", stating, "I initially played a bass line on the song, and Dr.&#160;Dre, Tommy Coster Jr. and I built the track from there. <a href="/wiki/Eminem" title="Eminem">Eminem</a> then heard the track, and he wrote the rap to it."<sup id="cite_ref-songwriter_universe_177-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-songwriter_universe-177">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup> This account is essentially confirmed by Eminem in his book <i>Angry Blonde</i>, stating that the tune for the song was composed by a studio bassist and keyboardist while Dr.&#160;Dre was out of the studio but Young later programmed the song's beat after returning.<sup id="cite_ref-184" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-184">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A group of disgruntled former associates of Dr.&#160;Dre complained that they had not received their full due for work on the label in the September 2003 issue of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Source_(magazine)" class="mw-redirect" title="The Source (magazine)">The Source</a></i>. A producer named Neff-U claimed to have produced the songs "Say What You Say" and "My Dad's Gone Crazy" on <i><a href="/wiki/The_Eminem_Show" title="The Eminem Show">The Eminem Show</a></i>, the songs "If I Can't" and "Back Down" on 50 Cent's <i><a href="/wiki/Get_Rich_or_Die_Tryin%27_(album)" class="mw-redirect" title="Get Rich or Die Tryin&#39; (album)">Get Rich or Die Tryin'</a></i>, and the beat featured on Dr.&#160;Dre's commercial for <a href="/wiki/Coors_Brewing_Company" title="Coors Brewing Company">Coors beer</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-source_2003_181-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-source_2003-181">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Although Young studies piano and music theory, he serves as more of a <a href="/wiki/Conductor_(music)" class="mw-redirect" title="Conductor (music)">conductor</a> than a musician himself, as Josh Tyrangiel of <i>Time</i> magazine has noted:
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942"><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Every Dre track begins the same way, with Dre behind a drum machine in a room full of trusted musicians. (They carry beepers. When he wants to work, they work.) He'll program a beat, then ask the musicians to play along; when Dre hears something he likes, he isolates the player and tells him how to refine the sound. "My greatest talent," Dre says, "is knowing exactly what I want to hear."<sup id="cite_ref-in_the_doctor&#39;s_house_171-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-in_the_doctor&#39;s_house-171">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>Although Snoop Dogg retains working relationships with Warren G and Daz, who are alleged to be uncredited contributors on the hit albums <i>The Chronic</i> and <i>Doggystyle</i>, he states that Dr.&#160;Dre is capable of making beats without the help of collaborators, and that he is responsible for the success of his numerous albums.<sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185">&#91;185&#93;</a></sup> Dr.&#160;Dre's prominent studio collaborators, including <a href="/wiki/Scott_Storch" title="Scott Storch">Scott Storch</a>, Elizondo, <a href="/wiki/Mark_Batson" title="Mark Batson">Mark Batson</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dawaun_Parker" title="Dawaun Parker">Dawaun Parker</a>, have shared co-writing, instrumental, and more recently co-production credits on the songs where he is credited as the producer.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Anderson_.Paak" title="Anderson .Paak">Anderson .Paak</a> also praised Dr. Dre in a 2016 interview with <i>Music Times</i>, telling the publication that it was a dream come true to work with Dre.<sup id="cite_ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ghostwriters">Ghostwriters</span></h3>
<p>It is acknowledged that most of Dr.&#160;Dre's raps are written for him by others, though he retains ultimate control over his lyrics and the themes of his songs.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEdwards2009229_187-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEdwards2009229-187">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup> As Aftermath producer Mahogany told <i>Scratch</i>: "It's like a class room in [the booth]. He'll have three writers in there. They'll bring in something, he'll recite it, then he'll say, 'Change this line, change this word,' like he's grading papers."<sup id="cite_ref-scratch_2006_188-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-scratch_2006-188">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup>
As seen in the credits for tracks Young has appeared on, there are often multiple people who contribute to his songs (although often in hip hop many people are officially credited as a writer for a song, even the producer).
</p><p>In the book <i><a href="/wiki/How_to_Rap" title="How to Rap">How to Rap</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/RBX" title="RBX">RBX</a> explains that writing <i><a href="/wiki/The_Chronic" title="The Chronic">The Chronic</a></i> was a "team effort"<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEdwards2009MC_189-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEdwards2009MC-189">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup> and details how he <a href="/wiki/Ghostwriter" title="Ghostwriter">ghostwrote</a> "<a href="/wiki/Let_Me_Ride" title="Let Me Ride">Let Me Ride</a>" for Dre.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEdwards2009MC_189-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEdwards2009MC-189">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup> In regard to ghostwriting lyrics he says, "Dre doesn't profess to be no super-duper rap dude – Dre is a super-duper producer".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEdwards2009MC_189-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEdwards2009MC-189">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup> As a member of N.W.A, the D.O.C. wrote lyrics for him while he stuck with producing.<sup id="cite_ref-doc_formula_23-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-doc_formula-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Jay-Z" title="Jay-Z">Jay-Z</a> ghostwrote lyrics for the single "Still D.R.E." from Dr.&#160;Dre's album <i>2001</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-changes_2001_44-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-changes_2001-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Personal_life">Personal life</span></h2>
<p>On December 15, 1981, when Dre was 16 years old and his then-girlfriend Cassandra Joy Greene was 15 years old, the two had a son named Curtis, who was brought up by Greene and first met Dre 20 years later.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERo200711_190-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERo200711-190">&#91;190&#93;</a></sup> Curtis performed as a rapper under the name Hood Surgeon.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERo200719_191-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERo200719-191">&#91;191&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1983, Dre and Lisa Johnson had a daughter named La Tanya Danielle Young.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_192-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-192">&#91;192&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:3_193-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-193">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup> Dre and Johnson have three daughters together.<sup id="cite_ref-194" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-194">&#91;194&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1988, Dre and Jenita Porter had a son named Andre Young Jr. In 1990, Porter sued Dre, seeking $5,000 of child support per month.<sup id="cite_ref-Andre_Jr_195-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Andre_Jr-195">&#91;195&#93;</a></sup> On August 23, 2008, Andre died at the age of 20 from an overdose of <a href="/wiki/Heroin" title="Heroin">heroin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Morphine" title="Morphine">morphine</a><sup id="cite_ref-196" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-196">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup> at his mother's <a href="/wiki/Woodland_Hills,_California" class="mw-redirect" title="Woodland Hills, California">Woodland Hills</a> home.<sup id="cite_ref-Andre_Jr_195-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Andre_Jr-195">&#91;195&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>From 1987 to 1996, Dre dated singer <a href="/wiki/Michel%27le" title="Michel&#39;le">Michel'le</a>, who frequently contributed vocals to Ruthless Records and Death Row Records albums.<sup id="cite_ref-197" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-197">&#91;197&#93;</a></sup> In 1991, they had a son named Marcel.<sup id="cite_ref-198" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-198">&#91;198&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERo200753_199-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERo200753-199">&#91;199&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In April 1992, after a verbal dispute with his engineer, Dre was consequentially shot four times in his leg.<sup id="cite_ref-200" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-200">&#91;200&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1996, Dre married Nicole (née Plotzker) Threatt, who was previously married to basketball player <a href="/wiki/Sedale_Threatt" title="Sedale Threatt">Sedale Threatt</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERo2007145_201-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERo2007145-201">&#91;201&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:3_193-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-193">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup> They have two children together: a son named Truice (born 1997) and a daughter named Truly (born 2001).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERo2007156,_204_202-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERo2007156,_204-202">&#91;202&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2001, Dre earned a total of about US$52&#160;million from selling part of his share of Aftermath Entertainment to <a href="/wiki/Interscope_Records" title="Interscope Records">Interscope Records</a> and his production of such hit songs that year as "<a href="/wiki/Family_Affair_(Mary_J._Blige_song)" title="Family Affair (Mary J. Blige song)">Family Affair</a>" by <a href="/wiki/Mary_J._Blige" title="Mary J. Blige">Mary J. Blige</a>. <i>Rolling Stone</i> magazine thus named him the second highest-paid artist of the year.<sup id="cite_ref-wealth_2001_49-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wealth_2001-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> Dr.&#160;Dre was ranked 44th in 2004 from earnings of $11.4&#160;million, primarily from production royalties from such projects as albums from <a href="/wiki/G-Unit" title="G-Unit">G-Unit</a> and <a href="/wiki/D12" title="D12">D12</a> and the single "<a href="/wiki/Rich_Girl_(Gwen_Stefani_song)" title="Rich Girl (Gwen Stefani song)">Rich Girl</a>" by singer <a href="/wiki/Gwen_Stefani" title="Gwen Stefani">Gwen Stefani</a> and rapper <a href="/wiki/Eve_(entertainer)" class="mw-redirect" title="Eve (entertainer)">Eve</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-wealth_2004_203-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wealth_2004-203">&#91;203&#93;</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Forbes" title="Forbes">Forbes</a></i> estimated his net worth at US$270 million in 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-204" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-204">&#91;204&#93;</a></sup> The same publication later reported that he acquired US$110&#160;million via his various endeavors in 2012, making him the highest–paid artist of the year.<sup id="cite_ref-205" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-205">&#91;205&#93;</a></sup> Income from the 2014 sale of <a href="/wiki/Beats_Electronics" title="Beats Electronics">Beats</a> to Apple, contributing to what <i>Forbes</i> termed "the biggest single-year payday of any musician in history", made Dr. Dre the world's richest musical performer of 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-forbes2_206-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-forbes2-206">&#91;206&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2014, Dre purchased a $40 million home in the <a href="/wiki/Brentwood,_Los_Angeles" title="Brentwood, Los Angeles">Brentwood</a> neighborhood of Los Angeles from its previous owners, <a href="/wiki/NFL" class="mw-redirect" title="NFL">NFL</a> player <a href="/wiki/Tom_Brady" title="Tom Brady">Tom Brady</a> and supermodel <a href="/wiki/Gisele_B%C3%BCndchen" title="Gisele Bündchen">Gisele Bündchen</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-207" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-207">&#91;207&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>It was reported that Dre suffered a <a href="/wiki/Brain_aneurysm" class="mw-redirect" title="Brain aneurysm">brain aneurysm</a> on January 5, 2021,<sup id="cite_ref-208" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-208">&#91;208&#93;</a></sup> and that he was admitted to <a href="/wiki/Cedars-Sinai_Medical_Center" title="Cedars-Sinai Medical Center">Cedars-Sinai Medical Center</a>'s ICU in Los Angeles, California.<sup id="cite_ref-209" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-209">&#91;209&#93;</a></sup> Hours after his admission to the hospital, Dre's home was targeted for an attempted burglary.<sup id="cite_ref-210" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-210">&#91;210&#93;</a></sup> He eventually received support from <a href="/wiki/LeBron_James" title="LeBron James">LeBron James</a>, <a href="/wiki/Martin_Lawrence" title="Martin Lawrence">Martin Lawrence</a>, <a href="/wiki/LL_Cool_J" title="LL Cool J">LL Cool J</a>, <a href="/wiki/Missy_Elliott" title="Missy Elliott">Missy Elliott</a>, <a href="/wiki/Snoop_Dogg" title="Snoop Dogg">Snoop Dogg</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ice_Cube" title="Ice Cube">Ice Cube</a>, <a href="/wiki/50_Cent" title="50 Cent">50 Cent</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ellen_DeGeneres" title="Ellen DeGeneres">Ellen DeGeneres</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ciara" title="Ciara">Ciara</a>, her husband <a href="/wiki/Russell_Wilson" title="Russell Wilson">Russell Wilson</a>, <a href="/wiki/T.I." title="T.I.">T.I.</a>, <a href="/wiki/Quincy_Jones" title="Quincy Jones">Quincy Jones</a> and others.<sup id="cite_ref-211" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-211">&#91;211&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-212" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-212">&#91;212&#93;</a></sup> In February, he was released with a following message on Instagram: "Thanks to my family, friends and fans for their interest and well wishes. I'm doing great and getting excellent care from my medical team. I will be out of the hospital and back home soon. Shout out to all the great medical professionals at Cedars. One Love!!"<sup id="cite_ref-213" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-213">&#91;213&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-214" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-214">&#91;214&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In December 2021, Dre finalized his divorce from Nicole Threat for a reported sum of $100 million of his estate.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Controversies_and_legal_issues">Controversies and legal issues</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Violence_against_women">Violence against women</span></h3>
<p>Dre has been accused of multiple incidents of violence against women.<sup id="cite_ref-RStone-Beating-1991_215-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RStone-Beating-1991-215">&#91;215&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-216" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-216">&#91;216&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-217" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-217">&#91;217&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-218" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-218">&#91;218&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On January 27, 1991, at a music industry party at the Po Na Na Souk club in Hollywood, Dr. Dre assaulted television host <a href="/wiki/Dee_Barnes" title="Dee Barnes">Dee Barnes</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Fox_Broadcasting_Company" title="Fox Broadcasting Company">Fox</a> television program <i>Pump it Up!</i>, following an episode of the show. Barnes had interviewed NWA, which was followed by an interview with Ice Cube in which Cube mocked NWA.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves2009110_219-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves2009110-219">&#91;219&#93;</a></sup> Barnes filed a $22.7 million lawsuit in response to the incident.<sup id="cite_ref-220" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-220">&#91;220&#93;</a></sup> Subsequently, Dr. Dre was fined $2,500, given two years' probation, ordered to undergo 240 hours of <a href="/wiki/Community_service" title="Community service">community service</a>, and given a spot on an anti-violence <a href="/wiki/Public_service_announcement" title="Public service announcement">public service announcement</a> on television.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKenyatta200127_221-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKenyatta200127-221">&#91;221&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-222" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-222">&#91;222&#93;</a></sup> The civil suit was settled out of court.<sup id="cite_ref-HReporter-Barnes-2015_223-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HReporter-Barnes-2015-223">&#91;223&#93;</a></sup> Barnes stated that he "began slamming her face and the right side of her body repeatedly against a wall near the stairway". Dr. Dre later commented: "People talk all this shit, but you know, somebody fucks with me, I'm gonna fuck with them. I just did it, you know. Ain't nothing you can do now by talking about it. Besides, it ain't no big thing – I just threw her through a door."<sup id="cite_ref-RStone-Beating-1991_215-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RStone-Beating-1991-215">&#91;215&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In March 2015, <a href="/wiki/Michel%27le" title="Michel&#39;le">Michel'le</a>, the mother of one of Dre's children, accused him of subjecting her to <a href="/wiki/Domestic_violence" title="Domestic violence">domestic violence</a> during their time together as a couple, but did not initiate legal action.<sup id="cite_ref-224" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-224">&#91;224&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-HReporter-Michelle-2015_225-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HReporter-Michelle-2015-225">&#91;225&#93;</a></sup> Their abusive relationship is portrayed in her 2016 biopic <i><a href="/wiki/Surviving_Compton:_Dre,_Suge_%26_Michel%27le" title="Surviving Compton: Dre, Suge &amp; Michel&#39;le">Surviving Compton: Dre, Suge &amp; Michel'le</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-226" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-226">&#91;226&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-227" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-227">&#91;227&#93;</a></sup> Dre threatened a lawsuit against <a href="/wiki/Lifetime_(TV_network)" title="Lifetime (TV network)">Lifetime</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sony_Pictures" title="Sony Pictures">Sony Pictures</a> and filmmakers of <i>Surviving Compton</i> in a <a href="/wiki/Cease_and_desist" title="Cease and desist">cease and desist</a>, but never ultimately took action.<sup id="cite_ref-228" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-228">&#91;228&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-229" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-229">&#91;229&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Interviewed by Ben Westhoff for the book <i>Original Gangstas: the Untold Story of Dr Dre, Eazy-E, Ice Cube, Tupac Shakur, and the Birth of West Coast Rap</i>, Lisa Johnson stated that Dre beat her many times, including while she was pregnant.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_192-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-192">&#91;192&#93;</a></sup> She was granted a <a href="/wiki/Restraining_order" title="Restraining order">restraining order</a> against him.<sup id="cite_ref-:2_230-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-230">&#91;230&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Former labelmate <a href="/wiki/Tairrie_B" title="Tairrie B">Tairrie B</a> claimed that Dre assaulted her at a post-<a href="/wiki/Grammy" class="mw-redirect" title="Grammy">Grammy</a> party in 1990, in response to her track "Ruthless Bitch".<sup id="cite_ref-231" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-231">&#91;231&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>During press for the 2015 film <i>Straight Outta Compton</i>, questions about the portrayal and behavior of Dre and other prominent figures in the rap community about violence against women – and the question about its absence in the film – were raised.<sup id="cite_ref-NYTimes-Apologizes-2015_232-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYTimes-Apologizes-2015-232">&#91;232&#93;</a></sup> The discussion about the film led to Dre addressing his past behavior in the press. In August 2015, in an interview with <i>Rolling Stone</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-RStone-BehindCover-2015_233-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RStone-BehindCover-2015-233">&#91;233&#93;</a></sup> Dre lamented his abusive past, saying, "I made some fucking horrible mistakes in my life. I was young, fucking stupid. I would say all the allegations aren't true—some of them are. Those are some of the things that I would like to take back. It was really fucked up. But I paid for those mistakes, and there's no way in hell that I will ever make another mistake like that again."<sup id="cite_ref-Rolling_107-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rolling-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-HReporter-DreAddresses-2015_234-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HReporter-DreAddresses-2015-234">&#91;234&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In a statement to <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i> on August 21, 2015, exactly two weeks after his album, <i>Compton</i>, was released, Dre again addressed his abusive past, stating, "25 years ago I was a young man drinking too much and in over my head with no real structure in my life. However, none of this is an excuse for what I did. I've been married for 19 years and every day I'm working to be a better man for my family, seeking guidance along the way. I'm doing everything I can so I never resemble that man again.&#160;... I apologize to the women I've hurt. I deeply regret what I did and know that it has forever impacted all of our lives."<sup id="cite_ref-NYTimes-Apologizes-2015_232-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYTimes-Apologizes-2015-232">&#91;232&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the 2017 film <i><a href="/wiki/The_Defiant_Ones_(TV_series)" title="The Defiant Ones (TV series)">The Defiant Ones</a></i>, Dr. Dre explained about the Dee Barnes incident again, "This was a very low point in my life. I've done a lot of stupid shit in my life. A lot of things I wish I could go and take back. I've experienced abuse. I've watched my mother get abused. So there's absolutely no excuse for it. No woman should ever be treated that way. Any man that puts his hands on a female is a fucking idiot. He's out of his fucking mind, and I was out of my fucking mind at the time. I fucked up, I paid for it, I'm sorry for it, and I apologize for it. I have this dark cloud that follows me, and it's going to be attached to me forever. It's a major blemish on who I am as a man."<sup id="cite_ref-235" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-235">&#91;235&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Second_divorce">Second divorce</span></h3>
<p>Dre's wife, Nicole Plotzker-Young, filed for divorce in June 2020, citing irreconcilable differences.<sup id="cite_ref-236" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-236">&#91;236&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-237" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-237">&#91;237&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-238" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-238">&#91;238&#93;</a></sup> In November 2020, she filed legal claims that Dre engaged in verbal violence and infidelity during their marriage.<sup id="cite_ref-239" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-239">&#91;239&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-240" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-240">&#91;240&#93;</a></sup> She also stated that he tore up their prenuptial agreement that he wanted her to sign out of anger.<sup id="cite_ref-241" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-241">&#91;241&#93;</a></sup> Dre's representative responded, calling her claims of infidelity and violence in their marriage "false".<sup id="cite_ref-242" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-242">&#91;242&#93;</a></sup> 
</p><p>Before being released from the Cedar-Sinai Medical Center, he was ordered to pay Plotzker-Young $2 million in temporary spousal support.<sup id="cite_ref-243" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-243">&#91;243&#93;</a></sup> Between the spring and summer of the year, Dre was ordered by the Los Angeles County judge to pay his ex-wife over $300,000 a month in spousal support.<sup id="cite_ref-244" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-244">&#91;244&#93;</a></sup> The $2 million extension request was also dismissed, due to insufficient claims.<sup id="cite_ref-245" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-245">&#91;245&#93;</a></sup> 
</p><p>In July 2021, Dr. Dre was ordered by the Los Angeles Superior Court Judge to pay an additional $293,306 a month to estranged wife in spousal support starting August 1 until she decides to remarry or "further order of the Court".<sup id="cite_ref-246" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-246">&#91;246&#93;</a></sup> Then, in August, the judge denied his wife's request for a <a href="/wiki/Restraining_order" title="Restraining order">protective order</a>, due to her being afraid of Dre after a snippet leaked on <a href="/wiki/Instagram" title="Instagram">Instagram</a> of him rapping about the divorce proceedings and his possible brain aneurysm earlier that February; in this snippet, he called his wife a "greedy bitch".<sup id="cite_ref-247" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-247">&#91;247&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-248" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-248">&#91;248&#93;</a></sup> 
</p><p>In mid-October, Dr. Dre was served more divorce papers, during his grandmother's funeral.<sup id="cite_ref-249" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-249">&#91;249&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-250" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-250">&#91;250&#93;</a></sup> That same month, Dre was officially deemed "single" by the judge.<sup id="cite_ref-251" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-251">&#91;251&#93;</a></sup> The financial owings in this case included expenses of Dre's Malibu, Palisades and Hollywood Hills homes, but not his stock in past ownership of Beats Electronics, prior to its sale to Apple in 2014.<sup id="cite_ref-252" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-252">&#91;252&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-253" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-253">&#91;253&#93;</a></sup> As of December 2021, the divorce proceedings have entered its final stages.<sup id="cite_ref-254" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-254">&#91;254&#93;</a></sup> 
</p><p>On December 28, the divorce was settled with Dre keeping most of his assets and income due to the prenuptial agreement, although he would have to pay a 9-figure settlement within one year.<sup id="cite_ref-255" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-255">&#91;255&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Copyright_lawsuits">Copyright lawsuits</span></h3>
<p>During the course of <i>2001</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">&#39;</span>s popularity, Dr.&#160;Dre was involved in several lawsuits. <a href="/wiki/Lucasfilm_Ltd." class="mw-redirect" title="Lucasfilm Ltd.">Lucasfilm Ltd.</a>, the film company behind the <a href="/wiki/Star_Wars" title="Star Wars">Star Wars film franchise</a>, sued him over the use of the <a href="/wiki/THX" title="THX">THX</a>-trademarked "<a href="/wiki/Deep_Note" title="Deep Note">Deep Note</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-lucasfilm_256-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lucasfilm-256">&#91;256&#93;</a></sup> The Fatback Band also sued Dr.&#160;Dre over alleged infringement regarding its song "Backstrokin'" in his song "Let's Get High" from the <i>2001</i> album; Dr.&#160;Dre was ordered to pay $1.5&#160;million to the band in 2003.<sup id="cite_ref-257" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-257">&#91;257&#93;</a></sup> French jazz musician <a href="/wiki/Jacques_Loussier" title="Jacques Loussier">Jacques Loussier</a> sued Aftermath for $10 million in March 2002, claiming that the Dr.&#160;Dre-produced Eminem track "Kill You" plagiarized his composition "Pulsion".<sup id="cite_ref-258" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-258">&#91;258&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-259" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-259">&#91;259&#93;</a></sup> The online music file-sharing company <a href="/wiki/Napster" title="Napster">Napster</a> also settled a lawsuit with him and <a href="/wiki/Heavy_metal_music" title="Heavy metal music">metal</a> band <a href="/wiki/Metallica" title="Metallica">Metallica</a> in mid-2001, agreeing to block access to certain files that artists do not want to have shared on the network.<sup id="cite_ref-260" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-260">&#91;260&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Another copyright-related lawsuit hit Dr.&#160;Dre in the fall of 2002, when <a href="/wiki/Sa_Re_Ga_Ma" class="mw-redirect" title="Sa Re Ga Ma">Sa Re Ga Ma</a>, a film and music company based in <a href="/wiki/Calcutta,_India" class="mw-redirect" title="Calcutta, India">Calcutta, India</a>, sued Aftermath Entertainment over an uncredited sample of the <a href="/wiki/Lata_Mangeshkar" title="Lata Mangeshkar">Lata Mangeshkar</a> song "Thoda Resham Lagta Hai" on the Aftermath-produced song "Addictive" by singer <a href="/wiki/Truth_Hurts_(singer)" title="Truth Hurts (singer)">Truth Hurts</a>. In February 2003, a judge ruled that Aftermath would have to halt sales of Truth Hurts' album <i>Truthfully Speaking</i> if the company would not credit Mangeshkar.<sup id="cite_ref-261" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-261">&#91;261&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other">Other</span></h3>
<p>On June 28, 1992, hours before midnight, a barbecue grill and an overfill of charcoal caused Dre's <a href="/wiki/Calabasas,_California" title="Calabasas, California">Calabasas</a> mansion to set on fire.<sup id="cite_ref-262" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-262">&#91;262&#93;</a></sup> Two firefighters who exhausted the fire were treated in the hospital for minor injuries.<sup id="cite_ref-263" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-263">&#91;263&#93;</a></sup> The fire caused over $125,000 in home damages.
</p><p>Dre pleaded guilty in October 1992 in a case of battery of a police officer and was convicted on two additional battery counts stemming from a brawl in the lobby of the <a href="/wiki/New_Orleans" title="New Orleans">New Orleans</a> hotel in May 1991.<sup id="cite_ref-264" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-264">&#91;264&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1993, he was convicted of battery after an altercation with a man who stood outside the front porch of his Woodland Hills home in front of the musician's girlfriend. He claimed that Dre broke his jaw as a result.<sup id="cite_ref-265" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-265">&#91;265&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On January 10, 1994, Dre was arrested after leading police on a 90&#160;mph pursuit through <a href="/wiki/Beverly_Hills,_California" title="Beverly Hills, California">Beverly Hills</a> in his 1987 Ferrari. It was revealed that Dr. Dre had a blood alcohol of 0.16, twice the state of California's legal limit. The conviction violated the conditions of parole following Dre's battery conviction in 1993; he pleaded no contest and was sentenced to eight months in prison in September 1994.<sup id="cite_ref-266" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-266">&#91;266&#93;</a></sup> He was ordered to pay a $1,053 fine and attend an alcohol education program.<sup id="cite_ref-267" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-267">&#91;267&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-268" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-268">&#91;268&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In November 2004, at the <i><a href="/wiki/Vibe_(magazine)" title="Vibe (magazine)">Vibe</a></i> magazine awards show in Los Angeles, Dr.&#160;Dre was attacked by a fan named Jimmy James Johnson, who was supposedly asking for an autograph. In the resulting scuffle, then-G-Unit rapper <a href="/wiki/Young_Buck" title="Young Buck">Young Buck</a> stabbed the man.<sup id="cite_ref-269" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-269">&#91;269&#93;</a></sup> Johnson claimed that Suge Knight, president of Death Row Records, paid him $5,000 to assault Dre in order to humiliate him before he received his Lifetime Achievement Award.<sup id="cite_ref-270" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-270">&#91;270&#93;</a></sup> Knight immediately went on <a href="/wiki/CBS" title="CBS">CBS</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Late_Late_Show_(CBS_TV_series)" class="mw-redirect" title="The Late Late Show (CBS TV series)">The Late Late Show</a></i> to deny involvement and insisted that he supported Dr. Dre and wanted Johnson charged.<sup id="cite_ref-271" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-271">&#91;271&#93;</a></sup> In September 2005, Johnson was sentenced to a year in prison and ordered to stay away from Dr.&#160;Dre until 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-272" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-272">&#91;272&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On October 30, 2015, Ruthless co-founder Jerry Heller filed suit against Dre, Ice Cube, Eazy-E's widow, Tomica Woods-Wright, director F. Gary Gray and <a href="/wiki/Universal_Pictures" title="Universal Pictures">Universal Pictures</a> for <a href="/wiki/Defamation" title="Defamation">defamation of character</a> and copyright infringement over the biopic, <i>Straight Outta Compton</i>. The lawsuit states that depictions of Heller in the film, portrayed by <a href="/wiki/Paul_Giamatti" title="Paul Giamatti">Paul Giamatti</a>, were wrongfully taken from an autobiography he wrote about his involvement with Ruthless and N.W.A.<sup id="cite_ref-273" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-273">&#91;273&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-274" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-274">&#91;274&#93;</a></sup> The case was taken to court in June 2016 where a judge criticized the filing, saying that the film was "approved to portray these facts in "colorful and hyperbolic" terms".<sup id="cite_ref-275" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-275">&#91;275&#93;</a></sup> On September 2, 2016, Jerry Heller died of a car accident, preceded by a heart attack.<sup id="cite_ref-276" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-276">&#91;276&#93;</a></sup> However, his lawsuit kept on through his legal team and members of his estate.<sup id="cite_ref-277" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-277">&#91;277&#93;</a></sup> In October 2018, the lawsuit was dropped, costing Heller's estate $35 million for punitive and $75 million for compensatory damages.<sup id="cite_ref-278" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-278">&#91;278&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On April 4, 2016, <a href="/wiki/TMZ" title="TMZ">TMZ</a> and the <i>New York Daily News</i> reported that Suge Knight had accused Dre and the <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles_Sheriff%27s_Department" class="mw-redirect" title="Los Angeles Sheriff&#39;s Department">Los Angeles Sheriff's Department</a> of a kill-for-hire plot in the 2014 shooting of Knight in club 1 OAK.<sup id="cite_ref-279" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-279">&#91;279&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-280" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-280">&#91;280&#93;</a></sup> Three months later, in July, Dre was reportedly detained by police after confronting a next-door neighbor in Malibu about a test drive.<sup id="cite_ref-281" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-281">&#91;281&#93;</a></sup> It was also alleged that he brandished a handgun on the neighbor, but no evidence would be linked and Dre was soon released.<sup id="cite_ref-282" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-282">&#91;282&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On May 8, 2018, Dre lost a name trademark filing to a <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania" title="Pennsylvania">Pennsylvania</a> <a href="/wiki/Gynaecology" title="Gynaecology">gynecologist</a> named Draion Burch, who previously filed a trademark petition in 2015 to use his nickname, Dr. Drai, which has the similar pronunciation.<sup id="cite_ref-283" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-283">&#91;283&#93;</a></sup> Then, on June 26, Dre and Jimmy Iovine were ordered to pay $25 million to former partner and creative designer Steven Lamar, who sued the two co-founders for $100 million in unpaid royalties for designing the early Beats headphone models.<sup id="cite_ref-284" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-284">&#91;284&#93;</a></sup> The lawsuit was filed in 2015 after news broke out of Apple's acquisition of the headphone brand a year prior.<sup id="cite_ref-285" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-285">&#91;285&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-286" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-286">&#91;286&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In August 2021, Dr. Dre's oldest daughter LaTanya Young spoke out about being homeless and unable to support her four children. She is currently working for <a href="/wiki/UberEats" class="mw-redirect" title="UberEats">UberEats</a> and <a href="/wiki/DoorDash" title="DoorDash">DoorDash</a>, and she also works at warehouse jobs. She is living in debt in her SUV while her children are living with friends. Dr. Dre has allegedly stopped supporting LaTanya financially since January 2020 because she has "spoken about him in the press".<sup id="cite_ref-287" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-287">&#91;287&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
